<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2050-7496/2013">J. Mater. Chem. A</a></span>, 2024, <strong>12</strong>, 14583-14594</div><h1 id="sect189"><span class="title_heading">A high entropy O3-Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode with reversible phase transitions and superior electrochemical performances for sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Arindam 
            Ghosh
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Arindam&nbsp;Ghosh (orcid.org/0000-0001-9014-7345) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-9014-7345"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">abc</span></sup>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Rashmi 
            Hegde
          
        
      </span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">abc</span></sup><span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Premkumar 
            Senguttuvan
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Premkumar&nbsp;Senguttuvan (orcid.org/0000-0001-8465-5896) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8465-5896"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">abc</span></sup>
      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">New Chemistry Unit, Jawaharlal Nehru Centre for Advanced Scientific Research, Jakkur, Bangalore, 560064, Karnataka, India. E-mail: <a href="mailto:prem@jncasr.ac.in">prem@jncasr.ac.in</a></span>
      <br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">International Centre for Materials Science, Jawaharlal Nehru Centre for Advanced Scientific Research, Jakkur, Bangalore, 560064, Karnataka, India</span>
      <br><a id="affc"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">c</span></sup></a><span class="italic">School of Advanced Materials, Jawaharlal Nehru Centre for Advanced Scientific Research, Jakkur, Bangalore, 560064, Karnataka, India</span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      19th February 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 29th April 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 30th April 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>O3-type Ni/Mn/Fe-based layered oxides are very promising cathode materials for Na-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their high redox potential of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small> and Jahn–Teller inactive centers (Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>). However, their electrochemical performances are inferior for practical applications due to complex multi-phase transitions and structural instability at high voltages, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering and transition-metal migration. Herein, we tailor a Li/Ti co-substituted high-entropy oxide (HEO)-Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFMTO) cathode material, which exhibits highly reversible O3–P3–OP2 phase transitions with enhanced structural stability. Accordingly, the HEO-NFMTO cathode retains 88% and 77% of its initial capacity after 200 and 400 cycles at 1C and 2C rates, respectively. The co-substitution strategy also enhances Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion diffusivity, resulting in good rate performances of NFMTO (102.5 and 78 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 2C and 5C rates). A full Na-ion cell with an NFMTO cathode and hard carbon anode is fabricated, which retains 62% of its initial capacity even after 1000 cycles at 0.5C rate. The co-substitution strategy holds enormous potential for designing HEO cathode materials for practical SIBs.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect279"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Since the commercialization of lightweight Li-ion batteries in 1991, they have been widely used in portable electronics and even applied in electric vehicles due to their high energy density and long cycle life.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> However, the uneven distribution of lithium resources in the earth's crust along with its soaring demand increases the overall production cost of Li-ion batteries. From this viewpoint, the analogous sodium-ion battery (NIB) emerges as a strong contender for large electrical grid storage systems due to safe handling, low-cost and earth-abundant sodium precursors.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a> However, some limitations like sluggish Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion kinetics and low-temperature performances need to be improved.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3</span></sup></a> The present research on Na-ion cathodes revolves around layered transition metal (TM) oxides, Prussian blue analogues and polyanionic frameworks.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4–7</span></sup></a> Among them, the TM oxides (Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>TMO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) are attractive for practical applications due to their ease of synthesis, scalability, and high specific capacity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4,8</span></sup></a> The layered TM oxides can be classified into different categories (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, O3, P2, P3 and O2), depending on the number of stacking layers present in a single unit cell and the type of coordination environment.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> For example, in P2 and O3 types, their unit cells consist of two and three TMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers with prismatic and octahedral sodium ion sites, respectively. In general, the P2-type oxides are preferred over their O3 counterparts due to the reduced diffusion barrier for Na-ion hopping, despite their lower intercalation capacity arising from lower Na content. In contrast, O3-type structures undergo milder irreversible phase transformations at higher voltages compared to a P2 phase.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Ni-based O3-type layered oxides are attractive due to their promising electrochemical performances. The O3-NaNiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode exhibits a higher average Na-ion intercalation voltage of ∼2.7 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> due to the operation of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox couples and reversible capacities of ∼114.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the voltage window of 4.0–1.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>. However, it undergoes several phase transformations during (de)sodiation processes, resulting in serious capacity decay.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> In subsequent work, Ceder <span class="italic">et al.</span> curtailed the upper cut-off voltage to 3.75 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> for limiting the rapid capacity fade, but the unavoidable Jahn–Teller (J. T.) distortion of Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> still causes structural instability and capacity decay during the long-term cycling.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> Alternatively, the structural instability issue was addressed in the Ni/Mn-based layered oxide systems, wherein Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> are the J. T. inactive centers. As an example, the NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode can deliver reversible capacities of 125 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the potential window of 3.8–2.2 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> with 75% capacity retention over 50 cycles.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> Yet, the <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD studies of NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> reveal a complex phase transition of O3–P3–P′3–P′′3 structures during the (de)sodiation reactions. To mitigate this, Fe was substituted in NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> which produces smoother voltage profiles with 95% capacity retention over 30 cycles and a reversible OP2 phase formation above 4.0 V.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> However, increasing the Fe-content compromises the specific capacity but enhances the cycling stability of a material. Likewise, for the NaNi<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode, 80% capacity retention over 100 cycles with an average voltage of 3.15 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> between 4.0 and 2.0 V at 0.1C was recorded.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,16</span></sup></a> More interestingly, the same cathode showed reversible O3–P3–O3′ phase transitions upon increasing the upper cut-off voltage to 4.3 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">It has been widely accepted that substituting electrochemically inactive elements in place of redox-active transition metals can alleviate multi-phase transitions and improve cycling stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18–27</span></sup></a> In the case of Na–Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>–Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>–Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>–O oxides, Ti<small><sup>4+</sup></small> can easily replace Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> due to their comparable ionic radii (<span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>Ti</sub></small><small><sup>4+</sup></small> = 0.605 Å and <span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>Mn</sub></small><small><sup>4+</sup></small> = 0.53 Å).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> Moreover, the formation of a stronger Ti–O bond compared to the Mn–O bond in the lattice inhibits interslab gliding at higher voltages and disrupts the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/vacancy ordering.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> With Ti-substitution, electron density on oxygen atoms increases which results in greater repulsion between adjacent oxygen layers and increases the inter-layer spacing,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21,30</span></sup></a> and thereby Na-ion diffusion becomes faster, and higher rate capabilities can be achieved. This can also increase the average voltage of the material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31,32</span></sup></a> The Ti-substituted NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode delivers reversible capacities of 135 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a good capacity retention (∼85% at 1C after 200 cycles) whereas the <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD measurement reveals highly reversible O3–P3–O3 phase transformation during the Na (de)intercalation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> Analogously, the Ti-substituted quaternary Na[Ni<small><sub>0.4</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.4−<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ti<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>]O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<span class="italic">x</span> = 0, 0.2, and 0.4)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> cathode displays an increased average insertion voltage (3.2 V), energy density (460 W h kg<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) and better cycle life compared to the unsubstituted ones.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> Similar to Ti<small><sup>4+</sup></small>, the incorporation of Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> into TM layers also enhances the stability of layered oxides.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> During high-voltage operations, it mitigates the J. T. distortion of Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, which leads to a suppressed monoclinic distortion (P′3) and ensures structural integrity. It also slows down the migration of TM-ions into the Na-layer and forms a very robust solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) with fluoro-based solvents. The Li-incorporated Na<small><sub>0.85</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.175</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.525</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode delivers a capacity of 130 and 160 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the potential window of 4.2–2.0 V and 4.5–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, respectively, with a smooth O3–P3–O3 phase transition.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36,37</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Introducing multiple cations in the TM-layer can stabilize the host structure and minimize the notorious TM-migration to the Na-layer. This strategy is the bedrock for designing high-entropy oxide (HEO) cathodes, where multi-element coordination can easily accommodate the local structural changes associated with the (de)sodiation process.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38–43</span></sup></a> HEOs are recognized as a solid-solution phase comprising five or more elements at the same crystallographic site with a configurational entropy (<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>conf</sub></small>) ≥ 1.5<span class="italic">R</span>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44,45</span></sup></a> Owing to the disordered arrangement of multiple elements, HEOs often showcase exceptional properties unlike conventional materials, including high fracture toughness, high strength, temperature endurance, superionic conductivity and outstanding cycling stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46–49</span></sup></a> Recently, there has been a growing interest among researchers in employing HEOs for energy storage applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50,51</span></sup></a> Furthermore, the application of HEOs in SIBs has tremendous possibilities as it can avoid the dependence on high-cost transition metals with improved electrochemical performances. For example, a high-entropy cathode like NaNi<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>0.05</sub></small>Cu<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Sn<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> showed 75% capacity retention after 500 cycles with highly reversible phase transitions during the charge–discharge process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Similarly, Na-deficient O3-Na<small><sub>0.83</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Sn<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>O<small><sub>2−δ</sub></small> with expanded O–Na–O slab spacing displayed ∼87% capacity retention after 200 cycles with enhanced Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion diffusions kinetics.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> Another six-component HEO cathode, O3-NaFe<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Sn<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, preserved 81% of its initial capacity after 100 cycles at 0.5C rate with an impressive rate capability (81 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 2C).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Along this line, herein, we attempt to incorporate lithium and titanium cations into Na–Ni–Fe–Mn–O oxide, which can lead to a five-element based-HEO cathode that can resolve the issues with irreversible phase transformation, transition metal migration, sluggish Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion kinetics and structural degradation. The Li/Ti co-substituted HEO cathode – Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFMTO) (<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>conf</sub></small> = 1.515<span class="italic">R</span>) (see eqn (S1) in the ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) was synthesized through a high-temperature solid-state method. With a unique combination of various (in)active redox elements, this O3-type HEO cathode experiences highly reversible phase transitions during cycling, which enables it to retain 77% of its initial capacity even after 400 cycles. The multi-cation substitution improves the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion diffusion kinetics with minimal volume changes, leading to superior electrochemical performance of the NFMTO cathode.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect446"><span class="a_heading">2. Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect449"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Synthesis of O3-Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFMTO)</span></h3>
        <span>The O3-Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (NFMTO) compound was synthesized through solid-state synthesis using sodium carbonate (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>) (≥99.5%, Sigma-Aldrich), lithium carbonate (Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>) (≥99.997%, Sigma-Aldrich), manganese oxide (Mn<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>) (≥99%, Sigma-Aldrich), nickel oxide (NiO) (≥99%, Sigma-Aldrich), iron oxide (Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>) (≥99%, Sigma-Aldrich), and titanium oxide (TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) (≥99%, Sigma-Aldrich) as precursors. Stoichiometric amounts of these precursors were mixed in a stainless-steel jar by planetary ball milling at 1080 rpm for 40 min. After collecting the mixture, it was heated at 600 °C for 5 h in air to ensure the removal of the carbonates and adsorbed moisture. Next, the sample was ground and pressed into a pellet, and it was refired at 850 °C for 24 h in air. After completion of the reaction at around 200 °C it was quickly transferred to an Ar-filled glovebox to prevent any air-exposure.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect476"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of the NFMTO compound was recorded using a Rigaku Smartlab SE diffractometer using Cu-Kα radiation (<span class="italic">λ</span> = 1.5406 Å) in the 2<span class="italic">θ</span> range of 10–90° in Bragg–Brentano geometry. Structural refinements were performed using Fullprof software. The morphology and the elemental distribution of the NFMTO cathode were investigated with a field emission scanning electron microscope (Zeiss, Gemini SEM 500, FEI Inspect F 50 operating at 10 kV). X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) was performed on the cathode materials collected at different states of charge (SOCs). The electrodes were sealed inside Kapton tape to avoid air exposure during the measurements. Spectra were collected at room temperature for the Ni–K edge (8383 eV), Mn–K edge (6589 eV), Fe–K edge (7162 eV) and Ti–K edge (5016 eV) in transmission and fluorescence mode at PETRA III (P65 beamline, operating at 100 mA and 7 GeV), DESY, Germany. The standard samples (LiNiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, NiO, Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, Fe<small><sub>3</sub></small>O<small><sub>4,</sub></small> Mn<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, MnO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) were mixed with boron nitride and pressed into pellets (10 mm diameter, thickness 0.9–1.1 mm). The dwell time for each scan was 180 s and two scans were collected for each sample. Normalization of XANES spectra was performed using ATHENA software. Elemental analysis was executed by using an Avio 220 max inductively coupled plasma (ICP) optical emission spectrometer, PerkinElmer.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect491"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>All the electrochemical measurements were performed in two-electrode Swagelok cells. To prepare the NFMTO cathode, a weight ratio of 80% active material, 10% carbon black (TimCal, C45), and 10% polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder were thoroughly mixed with a sufficient amount of <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl pyrrolidinone (NMP) to form a thick slurry, which was coated on a piece of carbon-coated aluminum foil and was dried under vacuum at 120 °C overnight. Thereafter, electrodes were punched into ∼10 mm diameter circular disks and the active mass loading of the electrodes were estimated to be ∼2–3 mg cm<small><sup>−2</sup></small>. We studied the electrochemistry of the NFMTO cathode in sodium half cells using 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>/ethylene carbonate (EC)<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">propylene carbonate (PC)<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (weight ratio of 4.5<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">4.5<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.0) + 3 wt% FEC as the electrolyte and sodium metal (Sigma-Aldrich, &gt;99%) as the anode. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun, O<small><sub>2</sub></small> &lt; 0.1 ppm, H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O &lt; 0.1 ppm) and evaluated in a battery cycler (BT-lab, Biologic) over different voltage ranges at different C-rates. For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) experiments, a current equivalent to a C/10 rate was applied for 10 minutes followed by a 1 h relaxation. Prior to the GITT experiments, the cells were cycled at C/10 for two cycles. <span class="italic">In situ</span> XRD measurements were conducted using a BRUKER D8 diffractometer with a Cu-Kα source in an <span class="italic">in situ</span> cell fitted with a Be window. The cell was cycled from 4.0 to 2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> at a C/10 rate. Full Na-ion cells were fabricated using NFMTO and hard carbon as the cathode and anode, respectively. The hard carbon anode was pre-sodiated at a current rate of 35 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> for four cycles before assembling the full cell. The capacity ratio between the anode and cathode was maintained at ∼1.25. The full cell, NFMTO‖HC, was cycled at 0.1C rate in the potential window of 4.0–1.5 V.</span>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect506"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>The XRD pattern and the crystal structure of the as-synthesized NFMTO are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a and b</a>, respectively. The XRD pattern can be well indexed with the <span class="italic">R</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> space group. Rietveld refinement reveals the presence of &lt;1% NiO impurity in the as-synthesized material (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>) which is often encountered in the syntheses of nickel-based sodium transition metal oxides.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32,52,53</span></sup></a> The obtained cell parameters of the NFMTO cathode are <span class="italic">a</span> = <span class="italic">b</span> = 2.9794(8) Å and <span class="italic">c</span> = 16.0204(8) Å with an interlayer spacing of NaO<small><sub>6</sub></small> and those of TM/LiO<small><sub>6</sub></small> are 3.103 Å and 2.237 Å, respectively. The corresponding crystallographic parameters are listed in Table S1 of the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Furthermore, inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) analysis reveals the composition of the NFTMO cathode to be Na<small><sub>0.987</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.112</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.296</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.104</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.247</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.252</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which is closer to the targeted composition. The morphology of the NFMTO cathode was examined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a> reveals that the cathode consists of hexagonal-shaped particles in the range of 1 to 5 μm. The EDS mapping confirms the uniform distribution of Ni, Fe, Mn, Ti, Na, and O in the as-synthesized material.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Rietveld refined X-ray diffraction pattern, (b) crystal structure, and (c) particle morphology with energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping of Na<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Li<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.1</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical sodium storage performance of the NFMTO cathode material was first investigated in the potential window of 4.0–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> at a 0.1C rate (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>). During the first cycle, it exhibits charge/discharge capacities of 132/123 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with a coulombic efficiency (CE) of 93%. The voltage profiles entail a short plateau at 3.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> followed by a slopping region until 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, which can be ascribed to the activity of the Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox couple.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,23</span></sup></a> The corresponding d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> profiles show sharp and broad redox peaks at ∼3.0 V and ∼3.56 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, corresponding to the O3 to P3 phase transformation and solid-solution formation, respectively. It is worth noting that a small kink in the d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> profiles is noticed closer to 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, which could be the onset of OP2 phase formation<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54</span></sup></a> and will be discussed later in the manuscript. Furthermore, we extended the upper cut-off voltage of galvanostatic cycling to 4.2 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> to enhance the Na storage capacity of the NFMTO cathode. As a result, a greater amount of sodium ions is exchanged compared to in the 4.0–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> window, leading to the first charge/discharge capacities of 150/135 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The corresponding d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> profiles display a prominent redox peak at ∼4.1 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> due to the P3–OP2 phase transition.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54–56</span></sup></a> Furthermore, we studied the cycling stabilities of the NFMTO cathode in the 4.0–2.0 and 4.2–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> potential windows at 1C and 2C rates. In the 4.0–2.0 V window, the NFMTO cathode retains 93% and 88% of its initial capacity after 100 and 200 cycles, respectively, at 1C rate (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>). It is worth mentioning that the NFMTO cathode displays comparable or even better capacity retention compared to the recently reported Ni/Mn-based HEO-type cathodes (Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Upon extending the upper cut-off voltage to 4.2 V, the same cathode displays slightly inferior capacity retention (84 and 78% retention after 100 and 200 cycles, respectively). Similar trends in the capacity retention are also observed at 2C rate (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>). The NFMTO cathode retains 77% and 66% of its initial discharge capacity in the potential range of 4.0–2.0 V and 4.2–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> at 2C rate, respectively after 400 cycles. The capacity decay occurring in the 4.2–2.0 V window can be ascribed to more P3–OP2 phase transition at higher voltages.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54–56</span></sup></a> The stacking defects of TMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers along the <span class="italic">c</span>-direction give rise to the OP2 phase.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a and b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles and (c and d) d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> plots of the NFMTO cathode in the potential window of 4.0–2.0 V and 4.2–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> at 0.1C rate respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a and b) Long-term cycling stabilities and (c) rate performances of the NFMTO cathode in different voltage windows. (d) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data collected after different charge/discharge cycles (inset: magnified view of the high-frequency region). (e) Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) profile of the NFMTO cathode (inset: calculated diffusion coefficient values during the charge–discharge process).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The cycling stability of the NFTMO cathode is further corroborated with the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>). The first small semicircle in the high-frequency region (Fig. S1a–d<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) corresponds to the impedance due to the electrified interface (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>IF</sub></small>) arising from adsorption/desorption and migration of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ions on the surface of the cathode,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit57"><sup><span class="sup_ref">57–59</span></sup></a> while the second semicircle in the high-mid frequency region denotes the charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) at the cathode/electrolyte interface. The slopping line at the low-frequency region represents the Warburg impedance (<span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>w</sub></small>) attributed to the diffusion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ions into the cathode material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60,61</span></sup></a> As the cycling proceeds, another semicircle was noticed in the high-mid frequency region of EIS spectra (Fig. S1b–d<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), due to the electrolyte decomposition, which in turn produces a passivation layer on the surface of the cathode and creates an additional resistance for the movement of ions and electrons across the cathode-electrolyte interface (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>CEI</sub></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60</span></sup></a> The EIS fittings show the increase in <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>CEI</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> values for the initial ten cycles (Table S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). However, these values stabilize after 50–100 cycles (Fig. S1c and d<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), explaining the robustness of the CEI and better cycling stability of the NFMTO cathode (93% retention after 100 cycles at 1C).</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Next, we checked the rate capability of the NFMTO cathode at various C-rates in the windows of 4.0–2.0 and 4.2–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>. The NFMTO cathode delivers discharge capacities of 122.5, 118, 113, 108, 102.5 and 78 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 and 5C rates, respectively, in the window of 4.0–2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>. However, the same cathode renders slightly lower capacities at higher C-rates upon increasing the upper cut-off voltage to 4.2 V (130, 116.8, 105, 96.5, 86.8, and 63 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 and 5C rates, respectively) (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The drop in the discharge capacities could be ascribed to the lower Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion mobility due to generation of more stacking faults at high voltages along with oxygen redox activity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit62"><sup><span class="sup_ref">62</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To examine the effect of Li/Ti co-substitution on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion intercalation kinetics, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) experiment was performed to determine the apparent diffusion coefficient of sodium ions (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small>) during the charge/discharge processes. The GITT voltage profiles of the NFTMO cathode and the calculated <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> for the charge/discharge processes are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a> and the schematic illustrations of single current pulses are shown in Fig. S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> At the start of the charging process, the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> is estimated to be in the range of 2.6 × 10<small><sup>−9</sup></small> to 4.5 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> until 3.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, which diminishes to 1.31 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> to 1.04 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> during the O3–P3 transition. As the charging proceeds through the slopping voltage region (3.2–3.93 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>), the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> is maintained in the range of 9.31 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> to 1.13 × 10<small><sup>−9</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Thereafter, we notice a drop in the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> values (3.48 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> to 2.72 × 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) as the voltage reaches 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> due to the formation of OP2. However, these values are higher than most of the reported values (10<small><sup>−11</sup></small> to 10<small><sup>−12</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,63,64</span></sup></a> which corroborates the enhanced rate performance of the NFMTO cathode at higher C-rates. Overall, we estimate that the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> values of the NFTMO cathode are in the range of 10<small><sup>−9</sup></small> to 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> during the charge/discharge process, which is at least one order higher than that of the reported HEOs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40,43,65,66</span></sup></a> The Li/Ti co-substitution in the HEO cathode enhances the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ion diffusion kinetics, rendering better rate capabilities at higher C-rates.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Inspired by the enhanced electrochemical performance of the NFMTO cathode, we performed <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD and <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XAS measurements to understand the changes in the crystal structure and oxidation states during the (de)sodiation processes in the 4.0–2.0 V window. Previous reports showed that binary layered oxides such as NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit67"><sup><span class="sup_ref">67</span></sup></a> undergo a series of phase transitions (O3–O′3–P3–P′3), while the ternary cathodes (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, NaNi<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.33</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> and NaNi<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit68"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68</span></sup></a> display alleviated phase changes (O3–P3–P′3) during the cycling in the potential window of 4.0–2.0 V. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> displays the <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD patterns of the NFMTO cathode during the first cycle. The refined lattice parameters from the corresponding XRD patterns are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>. During the removal of the first 0.18 moles of Na ions, the (003) and (006) peaks shift toward lower 2<span class="italic">θ</span> values (indicating increased interplanar <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing due to the repulsion between adjacent TMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers), whereas the (102), (102) and (104) peaks shift toward higher 2<span class="italic">θ</span> values due to the contraction in the <span class="italic">ab</span>-plane. As a result, the <span class="italic">c</span>- and (<span class="italic">a</span>/<span class="italic">b</span>)-parameters increase and decrease, respectively. During the subsequent Na-ion removal in the range of 0.8 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 0.77, another set of reflections appears at 2<span class="italic">θ</span> = 15.8, 32.0, 36.0 and 37.2° in addition to the O3 phase, which could be indexed with the P3-type structure. This phase transformation also hints at an inhibition for Fe-migration from the TM-layer to the Na-layer.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit69"><sup><span class="sup_ref">69–71</span></sup></a> Furthermore, the P3 phase remains stable until 3.93 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> (or <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.54 moles) with a gradual left shift in the (003) peak due to Na-removal from the structure. As the charging voltage reaches 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, a slight shift for the (003) peak to higher 2<span class="italic">θ</span> values with diminished intensity was observed, which corresponds to the OP2 phase.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit54"><sup><span class="sup_ref">54–56</span></sup></a> The overall changes in <span class="italic">a</span>, <span class="italic">c</span>, and <span class="italic">V</span> parameters during the first charge are estimated to be 3.08%, 4.87%, and 1.69% respectively, which is comparable to those of recently reported multielement HEO materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19,40,42</span></sup></a> During the subsequent discharge, the reverse order of the above-mentioned phase transitions is observed without any additional phases, indicating the complete reversibility of phase transitions. Furthermore, we observe highly reversible phase transitions of O3–P3–OP2 from the 2D contour plot of the <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD patterns collected during two successive cycles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Furthermore, we performed XRD and SEM measurements on the cycled NFMTO cathodes after 200 cycles. The XRD pattern of the cycled NFMTO cathode shows the retention of the original O3 structure regardless of the P3–OP2 transformation at high voltages (Fig. S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) which is different from what has been previously reported.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> The SEM images confirm the retention of particle morphology after extensive cycling. Overall, we find that the Li/Ti co-substitution not only suppresses the phase transitions starting from the O3 structure but also enables highly reversible phase transformations, leading to better structural stability during cycling.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Evolution of <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD patterns over time during the 1st GCD cycle, (b) corresponding changes in lattice parameters, and (c) 2D-contour plot for the 1st and 2nd GCD cycles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Next, to probe the charge compensation mechanism during the first charge/discharge cycle, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurements were performed on the pristine and cycled NFMTO cathodes. The change in average oxidation states of transition metal elements (Ni, Mn, Fe, and Ti) was analyzed using normalized X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a–d</a>). Comparing the XANES spectra of the reference compounds (<span class="italic">e.g.</span>, NiO, LiNiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Mn<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, MnO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Fe<small><sub>3</sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small>, Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>, and TiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>) it was confirmed that the pristine NFMTO cathode contains Ni, Fe, Mn, and Ti in +2, +3, +4, and +4 oxidation states, respectively. No apparent shifts in the edge energy lines of Mn, Fe, and Ti K-edges are observed (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a–c</a>), which suggests that no further oxidation occurs for these elements (<span class="italic">i.e.</span>, electrochemically inactive) during the charging process. But the decrease in the intensity of the edge energy line implies probable changes in the local environment.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13,23,72–74</span></sup></a> Moreover, the intensities of pre-edges increase as the voltage is increased to 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small> (see the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a–c</a>), confirming the distorted environments of (Fe/Mn/Ti)O<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra. The Ni K-edge shifts from 8341.31 eV to 8344.01 eV as the cathode is charged to 4.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, indicating the oxidation of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>. We estimate a removal of 0.5 moles of sodium during the first charge. Considering the electrochemical inactivity of Fe, Mn, and Ti, the extraction of 0.3 moles of Na ions can be ascribed to the concomitant oxidation of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> to Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and the remaining 0.2 moles is removed through the oxidation of Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> to Ni<small><sup>4+</sup></small>, with an estimated overall Ni oxidation state of +3.7 at the end of the first charge. The observed energy shift Δ<span class="italic">E</span> ≈ 2.7 eV matches with the expected oxidation state changes of the NFMTO cathode.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19,23,74</span></sup></a> As the cathode is fully discharged back to 2.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>, all the transition metal K-edges shift back to their original positions, indicating full structural reversibility during sodium (de)intercalation.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">Normalized XANES spectra of the NFMTO cathode at the (a) Fe K-edge, (b) Mn K-edge, (c) Ti–K edge, and (d) Ni–K edge collected at different states of charge (SOCs).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, to test the practical applicability of the NFMTO cathode material, a full Na-ion cell was assembled with a hard carbon anode. The hard carbon was pre-cycled before assembling the full cell. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a</a> shows the voltage-capacity profiles of the full hard carbon‖NFMTO Na-ion cell cycled in the potential window of 4.0–1.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> HC at 0.1C rate. It delivers a reversible capacity of 116 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small><small><sub>cathode</sub></small> at an average voltage of 2.9 V, leading to an energy density of 336.4 W h kg<small><sub>cathode</sub></small><small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The full cell also sustains higher rates and delivers capacities of 116, 93, 82, 70, and 58 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2C rates, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>). Most importantly, the NFMTO‖hard carbon cell retains 62% of its initial capacity even after 1000 cycles at 0.5C rate (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a>).</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta01137f-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta01137f/d4ta01137f-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Comparison of the GCD profiles of the full cell, cathode and anode. (b) The variation of capacity with different rates for a full cell in the potential window of 4.0–1.5 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> HC. (c) Long term cyclability of the full cell at 0.05C rate.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect792"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, we developed a Li/Ti co-substituted O3-type HEO cathode material for sodium-ion battery application. The HEO-NFMTO cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 123 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at an average voltage of 3.2 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>0</sup></small>. The <span class="italic">in situ</span> XRD measurement revealed highly reversible O3–P3–OP2 phase-transitions with enhanced structural stability whereas improved diffusion kinetics was observed through the GITT experiment (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> = 10<small><sup>−9</sup></small> to 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). As a result, the Li/Ti co-substituted HEO cathode displays good cycling stability (88% after 200 cycles at 1C rate and 77% after 400 cycles at 2C rate) and rate capability (78 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 5C) which are comparable or even better than those of the reported cathodes. Overall, the strategy of incorporating multiple metals into TMO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers for tailoring HEO is a promising strategy to achieve Na-ion cathodes with enhanced electrochemical performance.</span>
